packing algorithm for an origami magic trick author Uwe Schnell number 2 4b 52 3 year 2 4 text The problem of folding a sheet of paper so that a single straight cut produces a cut out of a certain figure can be formulated mathematically as follows Given a polygon with holes P and a rectangle R supset P find a flat folding of R such that the cross section of the folding with a perpendicular plane is the boundary of P The authors give an algorithm to solve this problem The strategy is to pack disks such that the disk centers induce a mixed triangulation quadrangulation respecting the boundary of the polygon The algorithm is based on results by R J Lang in Proceedings of the twelfth annual symposium on computational geometry Philadelphia PA 1996 98 1 5 ACM NY 1996 and M W Bern and article MR 2 1c 5132 reviews Epp Algo journal Mathematical Reviews publisher Amer Math Soc title Diameter and treewidth in minor closed graph families author Xiao Tie Deng volume 2 1c number 5132 year 2 1 text The main result of this paper is that any minor closed family has the diameter tree width property if and only if it does not contain all apex graphs graphs that become planar if a vertex the apex is removed This property guarantees that every graph in the family with diameter no more than D has treewidth no more than f D where f is a fixed function Consequently linear time approximation schemes for several optimization problems on these graphs are obtained as are linear time algorithms for subgraph isomorphism and induced subgraph isomorphism Dihedral bounds for mesh generation in Faster circle packing with application to nonobtuse triangulation author Frederic Matheus number 99d 52 18 year 1999 text Numerical computations regarding the discretization of Dirichlet problems in polygonal domains need to generate triangulations with right or acute angles The author considers here a recent work of Bern Mitchell and Ruppert which uses circle packings to generate such meshes and speed up a key step of their method in which circles are packed into a non simply connected polygonal region to connect its boundary components In the work of Bern et al this step was computed in O n log sp 2 n time The new method presented here leads to an overall speedup of a logarithmic factor which makes the whole algorithm work in total time O n log n This method for connecting the boundary components of a polygon relies on a combination of several known techniques such as Voronoi diagrams minimum spanning trees intersection graphs neighborhood system algorithms of Eppstein Miller and Teng and dynamic tree techniques of Sleator and Tarjan Faster construction of planar two centers techreport Epp TR 96 12 title Faster construction of planar two centers author David Eppstein address Soc title Finding the k shortest paths author Koduvayur R Parthasarathy number 99h 5 73 year 1999 text The k shortest paths problem is to list the k paths from a specified source s to a specified destination t in a digraph with minimum total length The author considers digraphs admitting self loops multiple edges and cycles without negative lengths and the paths are not necessarily simple Using an implicit representation of the paths the author presents in detail an algorithm with time complexity O m n log n k where n and m are the number of vertices and the number of edges of the digraph This also leads to an algorithm of complexity O m n log n kn for the k shortest paths problem from a given source s to all other vertices of the graph To get the implicit representation one starts with a single destination t shortest path tree T of the given digraph G with source s and destination t and produces a heap H sb G v at each vertex v leading to a directed acyclic graph D G and through that to a path graph P G with the property that there is a one to one length preserving correspondence between s t paths in G and paths starting from the root r in P G and finally to a 4 heap H G whose nodes correspond to the s t paths of G After developing a basic algorithm improvements are introduced in the heap construction to attain the above complexity The improvements in time complexity achieved through this algorithm when used for the dynamic programming applications to the following optimization problems are discussed at some length the knapsack problem sequence alignment inscribed polygons and genealogical relations Three open problems are suggested Finding the k smallest spanning trees inproceedings Epp SWAT 9 title Finding the k smallest spanning trees title Linear complexity hexahedral mesh generation author de Figueiredo Luiz Henrique number 2 a 68153 year 2 text One important problem in mesh generation is how to extend a given mesh on the surface of a solid to its interior Most results on such problems especially theoretical ones are restricted to the extension of triangular meshes to tetrahedral meshes However in practice quadrilateral and hexahedral meshes are often preferred because of their numerical properties Few theoretical results are known for this case For planar meshes it has been proved S Ramaswami P A Ramos and G T Toussaint Comput Geom 9 1998 no 4 257 276 MR 98k 68169 that a polygon with n sides can be meshed with convex quadrilaterals if and only if n is even Moreover O n Steiner points suffice as the vertices of the internal mesh and they can be found efficiently Thurston and Mitchell independently proved a similar result for spatial meshes but it is restricted to solids whose boundaries are topological spheres The resulting internal mesh is only topological not geometric in the sense that the hexahedral elements have curved boundaries and are not necessarily convex Moreover the size of the internal mesh is not linear in the size of the boundary mesh In this paper the author removes this restriction and proves that any polyhedron which is topologically a sphere and whose boundary is composed of an even number of quadrilateral faces can be meshed with a linear number of topological cubes The author has made some progress in extending this proof to geometric meshes only a finite number of cases are left to be tested and this could be done by machine but as he points out this extension would not be directly practical because the size of the interior mesh may be large albeit linear and its elements will be poorly shaped Maintenance of a minimum spanning forest in a dynamic planar graph techreport EppItaTam TR 9 title Maintenance of a minimum spanning forest in a dynamic planar graph 94 1999 no 446 388 433 MR 2 i 62 7 made the following two conjectures concerning the regression depth of points in d dimensional Euclidean space Conjecture 1 For any d dimensional set of n points there exists a hyperplane having regression depth lceil frac n d 1 rceil Conjecture 2 For any point set there exist a partition into lceil frac n d 1 rceil subsets and a hyperplane that has nonzero regression depth in each subset The authors prove the first conjecture and make some progress on the second The problems arise in the context of robust regression and have a natural geometric formulation The proofs are based on projective geometry and are consequently of a completely different nature from the usual proofs of theorems in the area of robust statistics The authors also trees author Fabrizio Luccio number 97c 5 52 year 1997 text Based on the fundamental sparsification technique devised by Eppstein Galil Italiano and Nissenzweig for the design of dynamic graph algorithms this paper shows how a planar graph can be efficiently processed under the insertions and deletions of edges that preserve planarity Although the definition of planarity crucially relies on the concept of plane embedding the present technique is applied independently of the embedding which may be altered by the graph changes The major results are a fully dynamic planarity test working in O n 1 2 amortized time per update or query where n is the number of vertices and various efficient dynamic algorithms to maintain connected components minimum spanning forests and 2 edge connected components all valid for planar graphs As an overall comment this is a significant and useful addition to the algorithmic theory of planar graphs Separator based sparsification II edge and vertex David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Giuseppe F Italiano and Amnon Nissenzweig journal J ACM publisher ACM volume 44 number 5 pages 669 696 month September year 1997 url http doi acm org 1 1145 26591 265914 review MR 98k 68 39 article MR 98k 68 39 reviews EppGalIta JACM 97 journal Mathematical Reviews publisher Amer Math Soc title Sparsification A technique for speeding up dynamic graph algorithms author Peter B Gibbons number 98k 68 39 year 1998 text This paper makes a major contribution to the study of dynamic graph algorithms that maintain some property of a changing graph more efficiently than recomputation from scratch after each change Dynamic algorithms can be classified as fully dynamic where both edge insertions and deletions are allowed or as partially dynamic where only insertions are allowed The improved performances for dynamic algorithms are achieved through the use of the new technique of sparsification which when applicable can speed up a T n m time bound for a graph G with n vertices and m edges to T n O n almost the time needed if G were sparse Sparsification involves partitioning the edges of G into a collection of O m n sparse subgraphs each with n vertices and O n edges The information relevant for each subgraph can be summarised in an even sparser subgraph called a sparse certificate Certificates are merged in pairs producing larger subgraphs which are made sparse again by computing their certificates The result is a balanced binary tree in which each node is represented by a sparse certificate Each update involves log m n graphs with O n edges each instead of one graph with m edges A more careful partition into subgraphs causes each update to involve a sequence of graphs with O n edges in total Three variants of the sparsification technique are developed The first is used in situations where no previous fully dynamic algorithm was known and gives time bounds of O n per update The second variant uses a dynamic data structure to maintain certificates possessing a stability property thereby transforming time bounds of the form O m sp p into O n sp p In the third variant deletions are performed as in the first variant and insertions by using a partially dynamic algorithm This leads to insertion times often matching those of known partially dynamic algorithms together with deletion times similar to those of the first variant Results include the maintenance of minimum spanning forests graph connectivity graph 2 edge connectivity and bipartiteness in time O n sp 1 2 per change 3 edge connectivity in time O n sp 2 3 per change 4 edge connectivity in time O n alpha n per change k edge connectivity for constant k in time O n log n per change 2 vertex connectivity and 3 vertex connectivity in time O n per change and 4 vertex connectivity in time O n alpha n per change Further results speed up the insertion times to match the bounds of known partially dynamic algorithms The paper concludes with sections on recent related work and remaining open questions Speeding up dynamic programming techreport EppGalGia TR 88 title Speeding up dynamic programming author David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Raffaele Giancarlo address New York NY 1 27 USA institution Columbia Univ Computer Science Dept number CUCS 327 88 year 1988 inproceedings EppGalGia FOCS 88 title Speeding up dynamic programming author David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Raffaele Giancarlo booktitle Proc 29th Symp Foundations of Computer Science publisher IEEE pages 488 496 month October year 1988 url http www ics uci edu eppstein pubs EppGalGia FOCS Applications volume 3 number 3 pages 1 27 year 1999 review MR 2 1b 5154 article MR 2 1b 5154 reviews Epp JGAA 99 journal Mathematical Reviews publisher Amer Math Soc title Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems author Paul Klingsberg volume 2 1b number 5154 year 2 1 text The subgraph isomorphism problem is the following Given a graph G and another graph H one must determine whether G has a subgraph that is isomorphic to H In this fully general form the problem is NP complete but if H is a fixed l vertex graph as the author points out this problem can clearly be solved in O n sp l time where n is the number of vertices of G In the current paper it is assumed that G and H are planar and in this case the author develops an algorithm that requires only O C sb Hn time although the constant C sb H grows exponentially with l necessarily so if rm P ne NP The main idea behind the algorithm is that of constructing a cover G sb i for a planar graph G and providing a so called tree decomposition for each element G sb i of the cover A tree decomposition of a graph G is a tree T with these properties 1 each node N of T is provided with a label L N subseteq V G 2 for each v in V G the set of tree nodes whose labels contain v forms a contiguous subtree of T and 3 for each edge v w of G there is at least one node N of T such that both v and w are in L N Specifically for an n vertex planar graph G the author shows how for any positive integer w to do the following in O w sp 2n time He covers G with a collection of subgraphs G sb i so that each vertex of G is included in at most w of the G sb i he provides for each G sb i a tree decomposition in which all labels have le 3w 2 elements and he partitions V G into subsets S sb i in such a way that for any connected w vertex subgraph H of G if i is the smallest value for which H cap S sb i is nonempty then H is a subgraph of G sb i but is not a subgraph of G sb j for any j i The main result an algorithm for deciding the isomorphism question in linear time is then based on dynamic programming in the trees associated to the subgraphs G sb i The author also uses this technique to construct efficient algorithms for a number of other decision and enumeration problems for planar graphs including connectivity diameter girth induced subgraph isomorphism and shortest path misc cs DS 9911 3 title RSA 2 journal Mathematical Reviews publisher Amer Math Soc title The minimum expectation selection problem author Marc Ulrich Stiller number 2 3m 68 54 year 2 3 text The authors discuss the min min expectation selection problem and variations selecting k out of n given discrete probability distributions to minimize the expectation of the minimum value resulting when independent random variables are drawn from the selected distributions They show NP completeness with a fully polynomial time approximation scheme if the number of distinct values in each distribution is a constant greater than 2 and NP hardness to approximate the problem within any constant approximation factor if that number is an arbitrary integer Finally they show that the related max min problem maximize the expectation is polynomially solvable for a constant number of values in each distribution The article is a very interesting example of demonstrating the interplay of probability theory and computer science The reader should be familiar with the concepts of NP theory including the issue of approximation algorithms for such problems The skip quadtree a simple dynamic data structure for multidimensional data Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{A disk-packing algorithm for an origami magic trick}},\r  author = {Uwe Schnell},\r  number = {2004b:52030},\r  year = {2004},\r  text = {\r     The problem of folding a sheet of paper so that a single straight cut\r    produces a cut-out of a certain figure can be formulated mathematically\r    as follows: Given a polygon with holes $P$ and a rectangle $R \\supset\r    P$, find a flat-folding of $R$ such that the cross-section of the folding\r    with a perpendicular plane is the boundary of $P$. The authors give an\r    algorithm to solve this problem. The strategy is to pack disks such that\r    the disk centers induce a mixed triangulation/quadrangulation respecting\r    the boundary of the polygon. The algorithm is based on results by R.\r    J. Lang [in Proceedings of the twelfth annual symposium on computational\r    geometry (Philadelphia, PA, 1996), 98--105, ACM, NY, 1996] and M. W.\r    Bern and D. Eppstein \\ref[Internat. J. Comput. Geom. Appl.  2 (1992),\r    no. 3, 241--255; MR1194449 (94e:52016a)].}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"A heuristic approach to program inversion\"><H2>A heuristic approach to program inversion</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-IJCAI-85\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{Epp-IJCAI-85,\r  title = {{A heuristic approach to program inversion}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  booktitle = {Proc. 9th Int. Soc.},\r  title = {{Diameter and treewidth in minor-closed graph families}},\r  author = {Xiao Tie Deng},\r  volume = {2001c},\r  number = {05132},\r  year = {2001},\r  text = {The main result of this paper is that any minor-closed family has\r    the diameter-tree width property if and only if it does not contain all\r    apex graphs (graphs that become planar if a vertex, the apex, is removed).\r    This property guarantees that every graph in the family with diameter\r    no more than $D$ has treewidth no more than $f(D)$, where $f$ is a fixed\r    function. The author considers here a recent work of Bern, Mitchell\r    and Ruppert which uses circle packings to generate such meshes and speed\r    up a key step of their method in which circles are packed into a non-simply\r    connected polygonal region to connect its boundary components. \r    \r    In the work of Bern et al., this step was computed in $O(n\\log\\sp {2}n)$\r    time. Computing},\r  publisher = {SIAM},\r  volume = {28},\r  number = {2},\r  pages = {652--673},\r  year = {1998},\r  url = {http://dx.doi.org/10.1137/S0097539795290477},\r  review = {MR-99h:05073}}\r\r@article{MR-99h:05073,\r  reviews = {Epp-SJC-98},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{Finding the $k$ shortest paths}},\r  author = {Koduvayur R. Parthasarathy},\r  number = {99h:05073},\r  year = {1999},\r  text = {The $k$-shortest-paths problem is to list the $k$ paths from a\r    specified source $s$ to a specified destination $t$ in a digraph, with\r    minimum total length. The author considers digraphs admitting self-loops,\r    multiple edges and cycles without negative lengths, and the paths are\r    not necessarily simple. Using an implicit representation of the paths,\r    the author presents, in detail, an algorithm with time complexity $O(m+n\\log\r    n+k)$ where $n$ and $m$ are the number of vertices and the number of\r    edges of the digraph. This also leads to an algorithm of complexity $O(m+n\\log\r    n+kn)$ for the $k$-shortest-paths problem from a given source $s$ to\r    all other vertices of the graph. \r    \r    To get the implicit representation, one starts with a single-destination\r    $(t)$ shortest path tree $T$ of the given digraph $G$ with source $s$\r    and destination $t$ and produces a heap $H\\sb G(v)$ at each vertex $v$\r    leading to a directed acyclic graph $D(G)$ and through that to a path\r    graph $P(G)$, with the property that there is a one-to-one &quot;length-preserving&quot;\r    correspondence between $s$-$t$ paths in $G$ and paths starting from the\r    root $r$ in $P(G)$ and finally to a 4-heap $H(G)$ whose nodes correspond\r    to the $s$-$t$ paths of $G$. After developing a basic algorithm, improvements\r    are introduced in the heap construction to attain the above complexity.\r    \r    \r    The improvements in time complexity achieved through this algorithm when\r    used for the dynamic programming applications to the following optimization\r    problems are discussed at some length: the knapsack problem, sequence\r    alignment, inscribed polygons, and genealogical relations. Three open\r    problems are suggested.}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Finding the $k$ smallest spanning trees\"><H2>Finding the $k$ smallest spanning trees</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-SWAT-90\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{Epp-SWAT-90,\r  title = {{Finding the $k$ smallest spanning trees}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  booktitle = {Proc. 2nd Scandinavian Worksh. Comp. Geom.},\r  review = {MR-2000a:68153}}\r\r@article{MR-2000a:68153,\r  reviews = {Epp-CGTA-99},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{Linear complexity hexahedral mesh generation}},\r  author = {de Figueiredo, Luiz Henrique},\r  number = {2000a:68153},\r  year = {2000},\r  text = {One important problem in mesh generation is how to extend a given\r    mesh on the surface of a solid to its interior. Most results on such\r    problems, especially theoretical ones, are restricted to the extension\r    of triangular meshes to tetrahedral meshes. However, in practice, quadrilateral\r    and hexahedral meshes are often preferred because of their numerical\r    properties. Few theoretical results are known for this case. For planar\r    meshes, it has been proved [S. Ramaswami, P. A. Ramos and G. T. Toussaint,\r    Comput. Geom. 9 (1998), no. 4, 257--276; MR 98k:68169] that a polygon\r    with $n$ sides can be meshed with convex quadrilaterals if and only if\r    $n$ is even. Moreover, $O(n)$ Steiner points suffice as the vertices\r    of the internal mesh, and they can be found efficiently. Thurston and\r    Mitchell independently proved a similar result for spatial meshes, but\r    it is restricted to solids whose boundaries are topological spheres.\r    The resulting internal mesh is only topological, not geometric, in the\r    sense that the hexahedral elements have curved boundaries and are not\r    necessarily convex. Moreover, the size of the internal mesh is not linear\r    in the size of the boundary mesh. In this paper, the author removes this\r    restriction and proves that any polyhedron which is topologically a sphere\r    and whose boundary is composed of an even number of quadrilateral faces\r    can be meshed with a linear number of topological cubes. The author has\r    made some progress in extending this proof to geometric meshes---only\r    a finite number of cases are left to be tested, and this could be done\r    by machine---but, as he points out, this extension would not be directly\r    practical because the size of the interior mesh may be large, albeit\r    linear, and its elements will be poorly shaped.}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Maintenance of a minimum spanning forest in a dynamic planar graph\"><H2>Maintenance of a minimum spanning forest in a dynamic planar graph</H2></A><A NAME=\"EppItaTam-TR-90\"><P>\r<PRE>@techreport{EppItaTam-TR-90,\r  title = {{Maintenance of a minimum spanning forest in a dynamic planar\r    graph}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and Giuseppe F. Hubert [Discrete Comput. Geom. 22 (1999),\r    no. 2, 167--176; MR 2000e:52024; J. Amer. Statist. Assoc. 94 (1999),\r    no. 446, 388--433; MR 2000i:62070] made the following two conjectures\r    concerning the regression depth of points in $d$-dimensional Euclidean\r    space: Conjecture 1. For any $d$-dimensional set of $n$ points there\r    exists a hyperplane having regression depth $\\lceil\\frac{n}{d+1}\\rceil$.\r    Conjecture 2. For any point set there exist a partition into $\\lceil\r    \\frac{n}{d+1}\\rceil$ subsets and a hyperplane that has nonzero regression\r    depth in each subset. The authors prove the first conjecture and make\r    some progress on the second. The problems arise in the context of robust\r    regression and have a natural geometric formulation. The proofs are based\r    on projective geometry and are consequently of a completely different\r    nature from the usual proofs of theorems in the area of robust statistics.\r    The authors also consider the computational aspects of the problems.}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Representing all minimum spanning trees with applications to counting and generation\"><H2>Representing all minimum spanning trees with applications to counting and generation</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-TR-95-50\"><P>\r<PRE>@techreport{Epp-TR-95-50,\r  title = {{Representing all minimum spanning trees with applications to\r    counting and generation}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  address = {Irvine, CA, 92697-3425, USA},\r  institution = {Univ. of California, Irvine, Dept. of Information and Computer\r    Science},\r  number = {95-50},\r  year = {1995},\r  url = {http://www.ics.uci.edu/~eppstein/pubs/Epp-TR-95-50.pdf}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Reset sequences for monotonic automata\"><H2>Reset sequences for monotonic automata</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-ICALP-88\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{Epp-ICALP-88,\r  title = {{Reset sequences for monotonic automata}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  booktitle = {Proc. 15th Int. Soc.},\r  title = {{Separator based sparsification I: planarity testing and minimum\r    spanning trees}},\r  author = {Fabrizio Luccio},\r  number = {97c:05052},\r  year = {1997},\r  text = {Based on the fundamental sparsification technique devised by Eppstein,\r    Galil, Italiano and Nissenzweig for the design of dynamic graph algorithms,\r    this paper shows how a planar graph can be efficiently processed under\r    the insertions and deletions of edges that preserve planarity. Although\r    the definition of planarity crucially relies on the concept of plane\r    embedding, the present technique is applied independently of the embedding,\r    which may be altered by the graph changes.\r    \r    The major results are a fully dynamic planarity test working in $O(n^{1/2})$\r    amortized time per update or query, where $n$ is the number of vertices,\r    and various efficient dynamic algorithms to maintain connected components,\r    minimum spanning forests, and 2-edge-connected components, all valid\r    for planar graphs. Italiano and Amnon\r    Nissenzweig},\r  address = {via Torino 155, 30173 Venezia Mestre, ITALY},\r  institution = {Univ. Ca' Foscari di Venezia, Dip. di Mathematica Applicata\r    ed Informatica},\r  number = {CS96-10},\r  month = {October},\r  year = {1996}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"EppGalIta-JACM-97\"><P>\r<PRE>@article{EppGalIta-JACM-97,\r  title = {{Sparsification --- A technique for speeding up dynamic graph algorithms}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Giuseppe F. Italiano and Amnon\r    Nissenzweig},\r  journal = {J. ACM},\r  publisher = {ACM},\r  volume = {44},\r  number = {5},\r  pages = {669--696},\r  month = {September},\r  year = {1997},\r  url = {http://doi.acm.org/10.1145/265910.265914},\r  review = {MR-98k:68039}}\r\r@article{MR-98k:68039,\r  reviews = {EppGalIta-JACM-97},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{Sparsification --- A technique for speeding up dynamic graph algorithms}},\r  author = {Peter B. Gibbons},\r  number = {98k:68039},\r  year = {1998},\r  text = {This paper makes a major contribution to the study of dynamic graph\r    algorithms that maintain some property of a changing graph more efficiently\r    than recomputation from scratch after each change. Dynamic algorithms\r    can be classified as fully dynamic, where both edge insertions and deletions\r    are allowed, or as partially dynamic where only insertions are allowed.\r    The improved performances for dynamic algorithms are achieved through\r    the use of the new technique of sparsification which, when applicable,\r    can speed up a $T(n,m)$ time bound for a graph $G$ with $n$ vertices\r    and $m$ edges to $T(n,O(n))$, almost the time needed if $G$ were sparse.\r    Sparsification involves partitioning the edges of $G$ into a collection\r    of $O(m/n)$ sparse subgraphs, each with $n$ vertices and $O(n)$ edges.\r    The information relevant for each subgraph can be summarised in an even\r    sparser subgraph, called a sparse certificate. Certificates are merged\r    in pairs, producing larger subgraphs which are made sparse again by computing\r    their certificates. The result is a balanced binary tree in which each\r    node is represented by a sparse certificate. Each update involves $\\log\r    (m/n)$ graphs with $O(n)$ edges each, instead of one graph with $m$ edges.\r    A more careful partition into subgraphs causes each update to involve\r    a sequence of graphs with $O(n)$ edges in total. Three variants of the\r    sparsification technique are developed. The first is used in situations\r    where no previous fully dynamic algorithm was known and gives time bounds\r    of $O(n)$ per update. The second variant uses a dynamic data structure\r    to maintain certificates possessing a stability property, thereby transforming\r    time bounds of the form $O(m\\sp p)$ into $O(n\\sp p)$. In the third variant,\r    deletions are performed as in the first variant, and insertions by using\r    a partially dynamic algorithm. This leads to insertion times often matching\r    those of known partially dynamic algorithms, together with deletion times\r    similar to those of the first variant. Results include the maintenance\r    of minimum spanning forests, graph connectivity, graph 2-edge connectivity\r    and bipartiteness in time $O(n\\sp {1/2})$ per change; 3-edge connectivity\r    in time $O(n\\sp {2/3})$ per change; 4-edge connectivity in time $O(n\\alpha(n))$\r    per change; $k$-edge connectivity for constant $k$ in time $O(n\\log n)$\r    per change; 2-vertex connectivity and 3-vertex connectivity in time $O(n)$\r    per change; and 4-vertex connectivity in time $O(n\\alpha(n))$ per change.\r    Further results speed up the insertion times to match the bounds of known\r    partially dynamic algorithms. The paper concludes with sections on recent\r    related work and remaining open questions.}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Speeding up dynamic programming\"><H2>Speeding up dynamic programming</H2></A><A NAME=\"EppGalGia-TR-88\"><P>\r<PRE>@techreport{EppGalGia-TR-88,\r  title = {{Speeding up dynamic programming}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Raffaele Giancarlo},\r  address = {New York, NY, 10027, USA},\r  institution = {Columbia Univ., Computer Science Dept.},\r  number = {CUCS-327-88},\r  year = {1988}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"EppGalGia-FOCS-88\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{EppGalGia-FOCS-88,\r  title = {{Speeding up dynamic programming}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and Zvi Galil and Raffaele Giancarlo},\r  booktitle = {Proc. 29th Symp. Foundations of Computer Science},\r  publisher = {IEEE},\r  pages = {488--496},\r  month = {October},\r  year = {1988},\r  url = {http://www.ics.uci.edu/~eppstein/pubs/EppGalGia-FOCS-88.pdf}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems\"><H2>Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-TR-94-25\"><P>\r<PRE>@techreport{Epp-TR-94-25,\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/9911003},\r  address = {Irvine, CA, 92697-3425, USA},\r  institution = {Univ. of California, Irvine, Dept. of Information and Computer\r    Science},\r  number = {94-25},\r  year = {1994},\r  url = {http://www.ics.uci.edu/~eppstein/pubs/Epp-TR-94-25.pdf}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"Epp-SODA-95\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{Epp-SODA-95,\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/9911003},\r  booktitle = {Proc. 6th Symp. Discrete Algorithms},\r  publisher = {ACM and SIAM},\r  pages = {632--640},\r  month = {January},\r  year = {1995},\r  review = {MR-95m:05178}}\r\r@article{MR-95m:05178,\r  reviews = {Epp-SODA-95},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  number = {95m:05178},\r  year = {1995}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"Epp-JGAA-99\"><P>\r<PRE>@article{Epp-JGAA-99,\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/9911003},\r  journal = {J. Graph Algorithms {\\&amp;} Applications},\r  volume = {3},\r  number = {3},\r  pages = {1--27},\r  year = {1999},\r  review = {MR-2001b-05154}}\r\r@article{MR-2001b-05154,\r  reviews = {Epp-JGAA-99},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  author = {Paul Klingsberg},\r  volume = {2001b},\r  number = {05154},\r  year = {2001},\r  text = {The subgraph isomorphism problem is the following. Given a graph\r    $G$ and another graph $H$, one must determine whether $G$ has a subgraph\r    that is isomorphic to $H$. In this fully general form, the problem is\r    NP-complete; but if $H$ is a fixed $l$-vertex graph, as the author points\r    out, this problem can clearly be solved in $O(n\\sp l)$ time, where $n$\r    is the number of vertices of $G$. In the current paper, it is assumed\r    that $G$ and $H$ are planar; and in this case the author develops an\r    algorithm that requires only $O(C\\sb Hn)$ time (although the constant\r    $C\\sb H$ grows exponentially with $l$---necessarily so, if $\\rm P\\ne\r    NP$). \r    \r    The main idea behind the algorithm is that of constructing a cover $\\{G\\sb\r    i\\}$ for a planar graph $G$ and providing a so-called &quot;tree decomposition&quot;\r    for each element $G\\sb i$ of the cover. (A tree decomposition of a graph\r    $G$ is a tree $T$ with these properties: (1) each node $N$ of $T$ is\r    provided with a label $L(N)\\subseteq V(G)$; (2) for each $v\\in V(G)$,\r    the set of tree nodes whose labels contain $v$ forms a contiguous subtree\r    of $T$; and (3) for each edge $\\{v,w\\}$ of $G$, there is at least one\r    node $N$ of $T$ such that both $v$ and $w$ are in $L(N)$.) Specifically,\r    for an $n$-vertex planar graph $G$, the author shows how, for any positive\r    integer $w$, to do the following in $O(w\\sp 2n)$ time. He covers $G$\r    with a collection of subgraphs $\\{G\\sb i\\}$, so that each vertex of $G$\r    is included in at most $w$ of the $\\{G\\sb i\\}$; he provides for each\r    $G\\sb i$ a tree decomposition in which all labels have $\\le(3w-2)$ elements;\r    and he partitions $V(G)$ into subsets $\\{S\\sb i\\}$ in such a way that,\r    for any connected $w$-vertex subgraph $H$ of $G$, if $i$ is the smallest\r    value for which $H\\cap S\\sb i$ is nonempty, then $H$ is a subgraph of\r    $G\\sb i$ but is not a subgraph of $G\\sb j$ for any $j&gt;i$. The main result,\r    an algorithm for deciding the isomorphism question in linear time, is\r    then based on dynamic programming in the trees associated to the subgraphs\r    $\\{G\\sb i\\}$. \r    \r    The author also uses this technique to construct efficient algorithms\r    for a number of other decision and enumeration problems for planar graphs,\r    including connectivity, diameter, girth, induced subgraph isomorphism,\r    and shortest path.}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"cs.DS/9911003\"><P>\r<PRE>@misc{cs.DS/9911003,\r  title = {{Subgraph isomorphism in planar graphs and related problems}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/9911003},\r  howpublished = {ACM Computing Research Repository},\r  month = {November},\r  year = {1999}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Subquadratic nonobtuse triangulation of convex polygons\"><H2>Subquadratic nonobtuse triangulation of convex polygons</H2></A><A NAME=\"Epp-TR-91-61\"><P>\r<PRE>@techreport{Epp-TR-91-61,\r  title = {{Subquadratic nonobtuse triangulation of convex polygons}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  address = {Irvine, CA, 92697-3425, USA},\r  institution = {Univ. of California, Irvine, Dept. of Information and Computer\r    Science},\r  number = {91-61},\r  year = {1991}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"Tangent spheres and triangle centers\"><H2>Tangent spheres and triangle centers</H2></A><A NAME=\"math.MG/9909152\"><P>\r<PRE>@misc{math.MG/9909152,\r  title = {{Tangent spheres and triangle centers}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {math.MG/9909152},\r  howpublished = {arXiv.org e-Print archive},\r  month = {September},\r  year = {1999}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"Epp-AMM-01\"><P>\r<PRE>@article{Epp-AMM-01,\r  title = {{Tangent spheres and triangle centers}},\r  author = {David Eppstein},\r  eprint = {math.MG/9909152},\r  journal = {American Mathematical Monthly},\r  publisher = {Amer. Lueker},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/0110011},\r  howpublished = {ACM Computing Research Repository},\r  month = {October},\r  year = {2001}}\r</PRE></A><A NAME=\"EppLue-RSA-02\"><P>\r<PRE>@article{EppLue-RSA-02,\r  title = {{The minimum expectation selection problem}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and George S. Lueker},\r  eprint = {cs.DS/0110011},\r  journal = {Random Structures {\\&amp;} Algorithms},\r  volume = {21},\r  number = {3--4},\r  pages = {278--292},\r  year = {2002},\r  url = {http://dx.doi.org/10.1002/rsa.10061},\r  review = {MR-2003m:68054}}\r\r@article{MR-2003m:68054,\r  reviews = {EppLue-RSA-02},\r  journal = {Mathematical Reviews},\r  publisher = {Amer. Math. Soc.},\r  title = {{The minimum expectation selection problem}},\r  author = {Marc Ulrich Stiller},\r  number = {2003m:68054},\r  year = {2003},\r  text = {The authors discuss the min-min expectation selection problem (and\r    variations): selecting $k$ out of $n$ given discrete probability distributions,\r    to minimize the expectation of the minimum value resulting when independent\r    random variables are drawn from the selected distributions. They show\r    NP-completeness (with a fully polynomial time approximation scheme),\r    if the number of distinct values in each distribution is a constant greater\r    than 2, and NP-hardness to approximate the problem within any constant\r    approximation factor, if that number is an arbitrary integer. Finally\r    they show that the related max-min problem (maximize the expectation)\r    is polynomially solvable for a constant number of values in each distribution.\r    \r     The article is a very interesting example of demonstrating the interplay\r    of probability theory and computer science. The reader should be familiar\r    with the concepts of NP-theory, including the issue of approximation\r    algorithms for such problems.}}\r</PRE></A><P>\r<A NAME=\"The skip quadtree: a simple dynamic data structure for multidimensional data\"><H2>The skip quadtree: a simple dynamic data structure for multidimensional data</H2></A><A NAME=\"EppGooSun-SCG-05\"><P>\r<PRE>@inproceedings{EppGooSun-SCG-05,\r  title = {{The skip quadtree: a simple dynamic data structure for multidimensional\r    data}},\r  author = {David Eppstein and Michael T.
